Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0007.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">It is always challenging to fabricate two-dimensional transition-metal dichalcogenides into multiple hollow micro-/nanostructures with improved properties for various potential applications. Here, hierarchically porous MoS<sub>2</sub>–C hollow rhomboids (MCHRs) have been creatively synthesized via a facile self-templated solvothermal approach. It has been clarified that the obtained MCHRs assembled beneath ultrathin γ-MnS and carbon cohybridized MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets under the structural direction of the MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O self-template. The prepared MCHR anode of sodium-ion batteries exhibited a reversible capacity of 506 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, ultrahigh rate capabilities up to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> with 310 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, and exceptional stability over 3000 cycles. This study provides inspiration for the rational design of hierarchically porous hollow nanostructures with specific geometries as an excellent electrode material for outstanding performance energy storage equipment.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D10402%26pageCount%3D8%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DLifeng%2BHan%252C%2BShide%2BWu%252C%2BZhe%2BHu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D12%26issueNum%3D9%26contentID%3Dacsami.9b21365%26title%3DHierarchically%2BPorous%2BMoS2%25E2%2580%2593Carbon%2BHollow%2BRhomboids%2Bfor%2BSuperior%2BPerformance%2Bof%2Bthe%2BAnode%2Bof%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D8%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D10409%26publicationDate%3DMarch%2B2020" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2020 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">
                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries"> Batteries </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291058&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Hollow structures"> Hollow structures </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292439&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Physical and chemical processes"> Physical and chemical processes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials"> Two dimensional materials </a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Mos2%E2%88%92c&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_MoS2%E2%88%92C">MoS<sub>2</sub>−C</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Hollow+Rhomboid&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_hollow%20rhomboid">hollow rhomboid</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Self-template&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_self-template">self-template</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Material&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20material">anode material</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The ever-increasing demands for high-energy density storage devices and ultrafast charge/discharge capability are prompting researchers to discover new strategies to advance battery chemistry and technology. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7" aria-label="reference 1 to 7">(1−7)</a> Among all the existing energy storage technologies, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have an obvious advantage of infinite sodium abundance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 8 to 10">(8−10)</a> However, challenges still remain because of the sluggish reaction kinetics and large volume changes compared to lithium chemistry, which are generally attributed to the larger radius of Na<sup>+</sup> (1.02 Å) than that of Li<sup>+</sup> (0.76 Å). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> Especially for anode materials in SIBs, the large volume change after full intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> can make the electrode pulverized, leading to a bad cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15 ref16 ref17" aria-label="reference 14 to 17">(14−17)</a> Therefore, a rational structural design of promising electrode materials is urgently required to overcome the existing drawbacks and promote the commercialization of SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The significant discovery of graphene exerts a spillover effect on the prosperous development of two-dimensional (2D) materials. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21" aria-label="reference 20 and 21">(20,21)</a> As a typical 2D material, molybdenum disulfide (MoS<sub>2</sub>) has attracted widespread attention for sodium storage because of its large interlayer spacing, high theoretical capacity (673 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>), and decent redox reversibility, which contributes to a stable electrochemical reaction and fast ionic transfer. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref24" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22 ref23 ref24" aria-label="reference 22 to 24">(22−24)</a> Nevertheless, MoS<sub>2</sub> faces inherently low electronic conductivity and large volumetric expansion during sodium intercalation/deintercalation. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25 ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 25 and 26">(25,26)</a> Lots of strategies have been attempted to overcome these problems. First, selecting cutoff voltage to avoid the collapse of the MoS<sub>2</sub> layer structure ensures the superior long cycle life. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref27 ref28 ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 4 and 27 to 29">(4,27−29)</a> Second, introducing carbonaceous materials can effectively improve the electronic conductivity and protect MoS<sub>2</sub> electrode from side reactions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30 ref31 ref32 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 30 to 34">(30−34)</a> Third, a hierarchical structure based on porous, hollow, and yolk–shell design can endow the robust kinetics and reduce the volume change to guarantee the high rate and long cycling performance. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 35 to 39">(35−39)</a> In these regards, fabricating 2D MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets into porous hollow micro-/nanostructures, meanwhile compositing with nanosized carbonaceous materials, should be the desirable way to further enhance the electrochemical performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub> anode. It is still a great challenge to prepare hierarchically porous MoS<sub>2</sub>–C hollow nanostructures with specific geometries by a facile and efficient method. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 40 to 42">(40−42)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Herein, hierarchically porous MoS<sub>2</sub>–C hollow rhomboids (MCHRs) have been fabricated via a facile self-templating method (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a>). Briefly, uniform and hierarchically porous γ-MnS and carbon cohybridized MoS<sub>2</sub> hollow rhomboids (γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C) are first prepared by a one-pot solvothermal reaction. Afterward, the MCHRs are obtained by 6 M HCl picking treatment at 700 °C for 2 h in the Ar/H<sub>2</sub> atmosphere (for details, see the Experimental Section in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). Owing to the unique hierarchical structure, the as-prepared MCHRs exhibit high reversibility, superior rate capabilities, and outstanding cyclic performance.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic diagram of the fabrication process of MCHRs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4">2.1.  Synthesis of Uniform and Hierarchically Porous MCHRs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The MCHRs were fabricated by a sample one-pot solvothermal reaction. Typically, 0.36 mmol of manganese(II) acetylacetonate (Mn(acac)<sub>2</sub>) and 0.18 mmol of molybdenyl acetylacetonate (MoO<sub>2</sub>(acac)<sub>2</sub>) were first dispersed in 8 mL of distilled water and 40 mL of isopropanol under vigorous magnetic stirring and kept for 2 h at ambient temperature. After that, 0.5 mmol of glucose was incorporated into the mixture and continuously stirred for 1 h. To the obtained claybank solution was then added 2.5 mmol of thiourea, and the mixture was stirred for another 4 h. Subsequently, the mixture was sealed in a 80 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and then heated at 200 °C for 12 h. Afterward, the sediment of the autoclave bottom was then centrifuged, dispersed in deionized (DI) water, centrifuged for several cycles, and dried at 60 °C for 12 h. The resultant powders were annealed at 700 °C for 5 h in Ar/H<sub>2</sub> and washed with diluted hydrochloric acid (6 M) and DI water to obtain hierarchically porous MCHRs. For comparison, single γ-MnS and MoS<sub>2</sub> samples were prepared according to the procedure given above, except that Mn(acac)<sub>2</sub> and MoO<sub>2</sub>(acac)<sub>2</sub> were used as only reactive reagents, respectively. The thermodynamically stable phase α-MnS and α-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C were obtained by annealing γ-MnS and γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C samples similar to the above process, respectively.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5">2.2.  Materials Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Powder X-ray diffraction data of the synthetized materials were carried out by using a Bruker D8 equipment. Field emission scanning electron microscopy (FESEM), high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM), and high-angle annular dark-field scanning TEM (HAADF–STEM) images were collected on JEOL JSM-7001F, JEOL JEM-2100HR, and FEI Tecnai G2 F20 microscopes, respectively. X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was acquired on Thermal Fisher ESCALAB 250Xi using a mono Al Kα (1486 eV) target. Raman spectra were recorded on a HORIBA spectrometer using a 532 nm laser. Nitrogen adsorption measurements were conducted on BELSORP-mini II, BEL Japan, Inc. at 77 K. Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) was performed by a TA Q100 thermal analyzer in air at a heating rate of 1 °C/min.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6">2.3.  Electrochemical Measurement</h3><div class="NLM_p last">All anodes were fabricated as follows: the as-obtained MCHRs (85 wt %), Super P (10 wt %), and polyvinylidene difluoride binder (5 wt %) were mixed together with an appropriate amount of NMP into a homogeneous slurry. Afterward, the resultant slurry was uniformly spread onto copper foils (thickness ∼200 μm) and continuously dried at 80 °C for 12 h. Finally, the coated copper foil electrodes (∼2.5 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>) were made into disks (diameter 12 mm). The CR2032 cells were fabricated in a glovebox using a Whatman GF/C separator and 1 M NaSO<sub>3</sub>CF<sub>3</sub> in diglyme electrolyte. The cycling and rate performance were carried out using a NEWARE multichannel measurement system within the potential range of 0.4–3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). The cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) analysis were performed on the CHI model 660E instrument. The preparation process of button cells of γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C and MoS<sub>2</sub> as active materials was the same as that of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C composites.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>, the pristine γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C composite transformed into an α-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C intermediate product after thermal treatment, accompanied by a slight upshift of the diffraction peak position from ∼9.0 to ∼9.6° in the (002) crystal plane of MoS<sub>2</sub> and the prominence of its characteristic peaks at a high angle region. After picking, the final MoS<sub>2</sub>–C product, namely, MCHRs, can well correspond to the hexagonally structured MoS<sub>2</sub> (2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, JCPDS no. 37-1492) but with a relatively weak and broadened peak feature, which indicates the disorder and expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> in MCHRs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43 ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 43 to 45">(43−45)</a> This is well consistent with the later electron microscopy observations.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. XRD patterns of pristine γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, annealed α-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, and final MoS<sub>2</sub>–C products, respectively (black diamond suit: γ-MnS, JCPS no. 40-1289, black circle: α-MnS, JCPDS no. 65-0891, black club suit: 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, JCPDS no. 37-1492).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a–g displays the SEM images of MCHRs. Low-magnification SEM image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a) displays the uniform rhomboids with a width of ∼600 nm and a length of ∼2 μm. Moreover, the enlarged SEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>b) and low-magnification TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c) clearly show the hollow morphology character of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C rhomboids. A closer examination (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>d) reveals that the walls of hollow rhomboids were made up of bended and randomly restacking carbon-hybridized MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets, which benefit from the molecular level affinity and in situ hybridization confinement effects of glucose with the metal center of sulfides. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46 ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 46 and 47">(46,47)</a> The HRTEM image in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e exhibits a <i>d</i>-spacing of 1.08 nm in the (002) crystal plane of expanded MoS<sub>2</sub>, which is the reason for the downshifted and broadened XRD peaks. The well-defined rings in the selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>f) further verified the polycrystalline nature of the MCHRs. HAADF–STEM and EDX mapping images of MCHRs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>g) indicate the even distribution of Mo, S, and C elements in a tube wall, but no Mn element was observed, indicating that Mn species were completely removed after acid etching. For comparison, detailed elemental mapping images and structural analysis of pristine γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C samples are also provided (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S1–S4</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a,b) SEM, (c) TEM, (d,e) HRTEM images of the MCHRs, (f) SAED patterns, and (g) annular dark-field STEM image and elemental maps of the MCHRs.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Raman and XPS characterizations were further conducted to analyze the detailed crystal structural composition and valence states of MCHRs. The typical Raman spectra display two different peaks at 377.8 and 401.1 cm<sup>–1</sup> in MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, assigned to the E<sub class="stack">2g</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> and A<sub class="stack">g</sub><sup class="stack">1</sup> vibration modes of 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub>, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>, red line). The downshift of the peak (bulk 2H-MoS<sub>2</sub> at 408 cm<sup>–1</sup>) indicates the few layers feature of the as-prepared MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> The peaks at 1358, 1584, and 2749 cm<sup>–1</sup> are assigned to the D, G, and 2D bands of partially graphitized carbon, respectively, indicating that the amorphous carbon shell was successfully formed on the surface of MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The intensities of these three peaks are obviously higher than that of the γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C samples without annealing (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>, black line), indicating that the heat treatment obviously increased the crystallinity, thus improving the electronic conductivity. TGA was conducted, and the carbon content of MCHRs is about 11.7 wt % (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b</a>). MCHRs show a higher specific surface area (∼42.6 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup>) than that of γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, and the former has a sharper pore size distribution (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a,b</a>), stemming from the formed pores by eliminating the Mn species. For XPS, as illustrated in Figure S5a–d in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, MCHRs are principally composed of Mo, S, and C from the survey spectrum. The C 1s XPS high-resolution curve (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5b</a>) reveals three peaks at 284.7, 286.7, and 288.9 eV for C–C, C–N/C–O, and C–O bonds, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> High-resolution Mo 3d spectra present two peaks at 229.6 and 232.8 eV for Mo 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Mo 3d<sub>3/2</sub>, respectively, suggesting the presence of Mo–S bonds in the composites (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5c</a>). There are two peaks for S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of S–Mo bonds at 162.5 and 163.6 eV (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5d</a>), respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To study the formation mechanism of MCHRs, the phase transition and morphology evolution of the intermediate products at different reaction stages were analyzed via XRD, SEM, and so forth. When quenching the solvothermal reaction at 1 h, uniform solid MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O rhomboid templates had first been self-assembled, as identified by the overall analysis from XRD, TG, XPS, and SEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S6–S8</a>, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S2</a>). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref50 ref51" aria-label="reference 50 and 51">(50,51)</a> With prolonged reaction time, during the sulfurization process, the surface of solid rhomboids became more and more rough for 2 h and was additionally coated with numerous nanosheets for 6 h. Only after 12 h, the hollow rhomboid structure and morphology of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–C composites are produced (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a–h). While in the early stages of the reaction from 1 to 2 h, the diffraction peaks of only MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O were observed. After 6 h, the newly wide diffraction peak located at 9.0°, the pristine but weak diffraction peaks located at 9.5 and 13.0°, and the newly strong diffraction peaks at 25.9° assigned, respectively, to the characteristic lattice planes of MoS<sub>2</sub>, MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O, and γ-MnS were simultaneously determined. The diffraction peaks of only MoS<sub>2</sub> at 9.0° and γ-MnS at 25.9, 27.7, 29.5, and 45.5° were retained, and all the diffraction peaks of MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O disappeared completely 12 h later. Therefore, evolution of the diffraction peaks of the MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O template at the corresponding reaction time is quite consistent with the SEM outcomes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>i). On the basis of the inside-out Ostwald ripening effect, we speculate that the mass transfers gradually from the inner to the outer surface of the solid templates and finally forms the γ-MoS<sub>2</sub>–MnS–C rhomboid structure. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> Hence, the corresponding morphology evolution strongly depends on the structure and morphology of the solid rhomboid MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O templates. Furthermore, the as-obtained hierarchically porous MCHRs were perfectly retained through harsh annealing and acid picking treatment, indicative of robust stability of such a hierarchical architecture. For comparison, in similar solvothermal reaction without addition of Mn source, yolk–shell structured MoS<sub>2</sub> control samples (labeled as MoS<sub>2</sub>) were prepared (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). While without the addition of Mo source, some large pure γ-MnS particles with agglomerated morphology were obtained by XRD and SEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S10</a>) and transformed into α-MnS, a thermally stable phase, after 700 °C thermal treatment (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a>).</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. FESEM images of uniform MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O rhomboids in the evolution process with reaction times of 1 (a,b); 2 (c,d); 6 (e,f); and 12 h (g,h) and (i) XRD patterns observed at the corresponding reaction time.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To evaluate the advantage of the unique structure and composition of HMCRs, the electrochemical properties were investigated. The galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of MCHRs in a cutoff voltage of 0.4–3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. The initial cycle has discharge and charge specific capacities of 650 and 589 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> [high Coulombic efficiency (CE) ∼90%], respectively. The capacity loss of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C is obviously exhibited in the first and second cycle, which may be attributed to the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) formation and parasitic reaction. Thus, the voltage plateau at 1.0 V is absent from the following processes except for the first discharge. Notably, voltage plateaus at 1.3 and 2.0 V are observed in subsequent discharge/charge cycles, corresponding to the reversible intercalation and deintercalation of sodium ions, respectively. The discharge/charge curves of the HMCR electrodes almost overlapped from the 3rd cycle over the 50th cycle to 100th cycle (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a), demonstrating a good cycle stability with a high reversible capacity (506 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>). <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S12</a> delivers CV curves of HMCR electrodes for the first four cycles at 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> from 0.4 to 3.0 V. As can be seen, except for the first cycle, the subsequent CV curves almost completely overlapped, indicating the high reversibility and stability of the MCHR-based electrode. This is consistent with the results of the literature. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52" aria-label="reference 52">(52)</a> During the 100th cycle, MoS<sub>2</sub>–C can deliver a stable capacity of 405 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b), whereas the γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C and MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes only deliver relatively low capacities of 268 and 205 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. The CE of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C electrodes with continued cycling was found to be stable at 99.8%, indicating negligible side reactions during each cycle. The enhanced capacity and cycling stability of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C compared with those of the other two samples should be attributed to the effective synergistic effects in MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets as well as ultrathin carbon layers. The rate capabilities of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, and MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes were also evaluated from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c). MoS<sub>2</sub>–C releases discharge capacities of 510, 435, 415, 405, 375, 324, and 290 mA h g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. At all current densities, the discharge capacity of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C is much higher than those of the other two samples. Furthermore, a specific capacity of 503 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can be achieved when the measuring condition is recovered to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 3000 cycles, a stable capacity of 265 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> can also be maintained (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S13</a>), indicative of its good structural stability. In fact, the integrality of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–carbon hollow rhomboid was still well maintained after 3000 cycles at 10.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). Such results corroborate that the as-prepared hybrids with a hollow structure and an expanded interlayer spacing of MoS<sub>2</sub> are robust enough to endure repeated Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation, leading not only to excellent long-term cyclability but also to superior high-rate capability. For comparison, the galvanostatic discharge/charge curves of the MCHR electrodes cycled between 0.1 and 3.0 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are also tested. Under the circumstances, the electrochemical mechanism in a Na–MoS<sub>2</sub> battery is proposed to be a conversion reaction (Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> + (4 – <i>x</i>) Na = Mo + 2Na<sub>2</sub>S). In the above reaction, the MoS<sub>2</sub> in the hybrid is totally converted to Mo metal and Na<sub>2</sub>S, resulting in sluggish kinetic properties and large volume changes, thus showing a bad electrochemical performance (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S15</a>). EIS of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, and MoS<sub>2</sub> is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S16</a>. The charge-transfer resistance of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C is much lower than those of the other two samples and remains the same after 100 cycles, which reveals that the MCHR hybrid structure stabilizes the interparticle contact resistance, resulting in a better cyclic performance.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical performance for SIBs. (a) Charge/discharge curves of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–C electrodes at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (b) cyclic performance of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, and MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (c) rate capability of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, γ-MnS/MoS<sub>2</sub>–C, and MoS<sub>2</sub> electrodes at different current densities (0.1–10 A g<sup>–1</sup>); (d) ultralong cyclic life of the MoS<sub>2</sub>–C electrode at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>; (e) CV curves of MoS<sub>2</sub>–C at various sweep rates (0.1–2.0 mV s<sup>–1</sup>) after 20 cycles; and (f) plots of log <i>i</i> vs log <i>v</i> and corresponding linear fitting.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To further study the kinetic property of MCHRs during the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/de-intercalation, the CV curves at various scanning rates were investigated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>e). The typical (peak 1 and peak 2) cathodic/anodic (peak 3 and peak 4) peaks correspond to the Na<sup>+</sup> deintercalation/intercalation reaction. These redox peaks can fit in well with stable discharge/charge plateaus in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a. Importantly, all CV curves form similar shapes as the scan rate increases, indicating the good reversibility and rate capabilities of MCHRs. The electrochemical reactions occurring on the electrode materials are generally involved in the controlled processes of diffusion and surface (pseudocapacitive behavior); the former can usually be found in the bulk materials. The latter often exists in nanoparticle active materials with larger interlayer spacing and/or large specific surface area. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref53" aria-label="reference 53">(53)</a> Both processes can be determined by the relationship of peak current (<i>i</i>) versus scan rate (<i>v</i>) in the following equations<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span>where <i>i</i> is the current density, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, and <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are the adjustment parameters. In particular, for the <i>b</i> value of ∼0.5, the electrochemical reaction is a diffusion-controlled process. With the <i>b</i> value close to 1.0, a surface-controlled process is dominant. When 0.5 &lt; <i>b</i> &lt; 1.0, the electrochemical reaction relies on both the above-mentioned processes, and the proportion of pseudocapacitive behavior is enhanced with an increase in the <i>b</i> value. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref54 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 54 and 55">(54,55)</a> In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f, all the <i>b</i> values calculated for MCHRs based on the anodic/cathodic peaks are close to 1, indicative of a pseudocapacitance-controlled behavior, which result in the fast Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/deintercalation processes and good cycle performance. Some similar observations were reported in previous studies on SIBs. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28 ref56" aria-label="reference 28 and 56">(28,56)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To further confirm the ultrastability of MCHRs, the microstructure and morphology changes of the active electrode materials after different cycle times were studied. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a after the 10th cycle, the MoS<sub>2</sub> lamellar structure is still preserved and similar to the original MoS<sub>2</sub>. The MoS<sub>2</sub> layers disclose an extended interlayer spacing when cycled for 500 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>b). After 1000 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c), the MoS<sub>2</sub> layers are further expanded and still flexible, in which single and bilayer MoS<sub>2</sub> are also observed. Furthermore, the overall morphology of MCHRs remains the same as that of the hollow rhomboid structure (see insets in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a–c and SEM in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S14</a>). This is different from Li–MoS<sub>2</sub> or Na–MoS<sub>2</sub> batteries at a cutoff voltage of 0.1 V, where the intercalated MoS<sub>2</sub> species (Li<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>MoS<sub>2</sub>) can be further transformed into Mo and Na<sub>2</sub>S. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> With the lower limit of voltage increasing to 0.4 V, a reversible intercalation/deintercalation mechanism is predominant and greatly avoids the conversion reaction step. Therefore, the created void space with a carbon hybrid can accommodate volume expansion of MoS<sub>2</sub> upon sodiation. In addition, the carbon hybrid can effectively stabilize MoS<sub>2</sub> and a small amount of conversion reaction product Mo and roughening of the Mo nanoparticles is greatly restrained, resulting in a good reversibility of Mo<sup>0</sup>/Na<sub>2</sub>S during the charge/discharge cycles, hence effectively alleviating the capacity fading. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> During the cycling process, apart from improving the conductivity, the carbon layers can also contribute to form a stable SEI layer. It is noticed that the porous structure of the MCHR, constituted of small nanosheets, provides a short diffusion path for Na<sup>+</sup> and electrons and large electrolyte/electrode contact area, which further promotes the sodium storage and maintains the original structure.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/medium/am9b21365_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. HRTEM images of electrode active materials after (a) 10, (b) 500, and (c) 1000 cycles under the charged state of 2.8 V in the Na||MoS<sub>2</sub>–C battery (insets are overview of active materials).</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/asset/images/large/am9b21365_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.9b21365&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">4.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a sample one-pot approach has been exploited to fabricate MCHRs. A key feature of this new method is that manganese molybdate MnMoO<sub>4</sub>·0.49H<sub>2</sub>O serves as the self-template with a solid rhombohedral crystal structure and morphology, which allows controllable growth of ultrathin γ-MnS–carbon-cohybridized MoS<sub>2</sub> nanosheets into a hollow rhomboid. As a result of the unique hollow architectures, the MCHR manifested improved sodium storage properties with a superior reversible specific capacity of 506 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, an ultrahigh rate capability of 310 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>, and an exceptionally stable cyclic performance of 265 mA h g<sup>–1</sup> after 3000 cycles at 10 A g<sup>–1</sup>. This strategy presented in this work may shed light on the rational design and construction of other metal dichalcogenide-based building blocks for energy storage and other applications.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b21365?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.9b21365</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Detailed experimental mechanism analysis, additional FESEM, TEM, XRD patterns, EDX data, BET, Raman spectra, TGA spectra, XPS, EIS, and the SIB CV profile of MCHRs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.9b21365/suppl_file/am9b21365_si_001.pdf">am9b21365_si_001.pdf (1.26 MB)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.9b21365" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The increasing interest in energy storage for the grid can be attributed to multiple factors, including the capital costs of managing peak demands, the investments needed for grid reliability, and the integration of renewable energy sources.  Although existing energy storage is dominated by pumped hydroelec., there is the recognition that battery systems can offer a no. of high-value opportunities, provided that lower costs can be obtained.  The battery systems reviewed here include sodium-sulfur batteries that are com. available for grid applications, redox-flow batteries that offer low cost, and lithium-ion batteries whose development for com. electronics and elec. vehicles is being applied to grid storage.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref2" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1038/nenergy.2016.128"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Aurbach, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">McCloskey, B.                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Advances in understanding mechanisms underpinning lithium-air batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Aurbach, Doron; McCloskey, Bryan D.; Nazar, Linda F.; Bruce, Peter G.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  The rechargeable lithium-air battery has the highest theor. specific energy of any rechargeable battery and could transform energy storage if a practical device could be realized.  At the fundamental level, little was known about the reactions and processes that take place in the battery, representing a significant barrier to progress.  Here, we review recent advances in understanding the chem. and electrochem. that govern the operation of the lithium-air battery, esp. the reactions at the cathode.  The mechanisms of O2 redn. to Li2O2 on discharge and the reverse process on charge are discussed in detail, as are their consequences for the rate and capacity of the battery.  The various parasitic reactions involving the cathode and electrolyte during discharge and charge are also considered.  We also provide views on understanding the stability of the cathode and electrolyte and examine design principles for better lithium-air batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref3" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201803609"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.                         cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">MoS2 Nanoflowers with Expanded Interlayers as High-Performance Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hu, Zhe; Wang, Lixiu; Zhang, Kai; Wang, Jianbin; Cheng, Fangyi; Tao, Zhanliang; Chen, Jun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">53</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">47</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">12794-12798</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ACIEF5</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Wiley-VCH Verlag GmbH &amp; Co. KGaA</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayer spacing of the (002) plane were synthesized and used as high-performance anode in Na-ion batteries.  By controlling the cut-off voltage to the range of 0.4-3 V, an intercalation mechanism rather than a conversion reaction is taking place.  The MoS2 nanoflower electrode shows high discharge capacities of 350 mAh g-1 at 0.05 A g-1, 300 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1, and 195 mAh g-1 at 10 A g-1.  An initial capacity increase with cycling is caused by peeling off MoS2 layers, which produces more active sites for Na+ storage.  The stripping of MoS2 layers occurring in charge/discharge cycling contributes to the enhanced kinetics and low energy barrier for the intercalation of Na+ ions.  The electrochem. reaction is mainly controlled by the capacitive process, which facilitates the high-rate capability.  Therefore, MoS2 nanoflowers with expanded interlayers hold promise for rechargeable Na-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/c7ta06577a"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sheng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Y.-E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Oriented SnS Nanoflakes Bound on S-Doped N-Rich Carbon Nanosheets with a Rapid Pseudocapacitive Response as High-Rate Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>J.                 10.1039/c7ta06577a</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D5%26publication_year%3D2017%26pages%3D19745-19751%26journal%3DJ.%2BMater.%2BChem.%2BA%26author%3DJ.%2BSheng%26author%3DL.%2BYang%26author%3DY.-E.%2BZhu%26author%3DF.%2BLi%26author%3DY.%2BZhang%26author%3DZ.%2BZhou%26title%3DOriented%2BSnS%2BNanoflakes%2BBound%2Bon%2BS-Doped%2BN-Rich%2BCarbon%2BNanosheets%2Bwith%2Ba%2BRapid%2BPseudocapacitive%2BResponse%2Bas%2BHigh-Rate%2BAnodes%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1039%252Fc7ta06577a&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b21365&amp;doiOfLink=10.1039%2Fc7ta06577a&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Oriented SnS nanoflakes bound on S-doped N-rich carbon nanosheets with a rapid pseudocapacitive response as high-rate anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sheng, Jian; Yang, Leping; Zhu, Yuan-En; Li, Feng; Zhang, Yue; Zhou, Zhen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">5</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">37</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">19745-19751</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JMCAET</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2050-7496</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">By virtue of abundant sodium resources and low cost, sodium-ion batteries have been considered as a promising candidate compared with the prevailing lithium-ion batteries.  However, substantial vol. changes and sluggish sodiation kinetics limit their practical application.  Here, we designed and prepd. a hybrid architecture of oriented tin(IIi) sulfide nanoflakes bound on S-doped N-rich carbon nanosheets (SnS/CNS) via a facile sol-gel and hydrothermal route.  The functional carbon nanosheets not only strengthen the interaction with SnS, but also enhance the cond. and pseudocapacitance of the composite.  This unique SnS/CNS anode delivers a high reversible capacity of 654 mA h g-1 and excellent rate capabilities of 487 and 250.7 mA h g-1 at current densities of 1 and 20 A g-1, resp.  Further kinetic analyses reveal that the pseudocapacitive contribution accounts for fast Na+ storage at high rates.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1038/s41467-019-09170-5"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hua, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cortie, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hu, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Indris, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chou, S.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dou, S.-X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">NASICON-Type Air-Stable and All-Climate Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries with Low Cost and High-Power Density</span>. <i>Nat. Commun.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>10</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">1480</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">1490</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1038/s41467-019-09170-5</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D10%26publication_year%3D2019%26pages%3D1480-1490%26journal%3DNat.%2BCommun.%26author%3DM.%2BChen%26author%3DW.%2BHua%26author%3DJ.%2BXiao%26author%3DD.%2BCortie%26author%3DW.%2BChen%26author%3DE.%2BWang%26author%3DZ.%2BHu%26author%3DQ.%2BGu%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26author%3DS.%2BIndris%26author%3DS.-L.%2BChou%26author%3DS.-X.%2BDou%26title%3DNASICON-Type%2BAir-Stable%2Band%2BAll-Climate%2BCathode%2Bfor%2BSodium-Ion%2BBatteries%2Bwith%2BLow%2BCost%2Band%2BHigh-Power%2BDensity%26doi%3D10.1038%252Fs41467-019-09170-5&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.9b21365&amp;doiOfLink=10.1038%2Fs41467-019-09170-5&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">NASICON-type air-stable and all-climate cathode for sodium-ion batteries with low cost and high-power density</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Chen Mingzhe; Cortie David; Wang Enhui; Hu Zhe; Wang Xiaolin; Chou Shu-Lei; Dou Shi-Xue; Hua Weibo; Indris Sylvio; Xiao Jin; Xiao Jin; Chen Weihua; Wang Enhui; Gu Qinfen</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature communications</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1480</span>
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn"></span>.
    </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of low-cost and long-lasting all-climate cathode materials for the sodium ion battery has been one of the key issues for the success of large-scale energy storage.  One option is the utilization of earth-abundant elements such as iron.  Here, we synthesize a NASICON-type tuneable Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7)/C nanocomposite which shows both excellent rate performance and outstanding cycling stability over more than 4400 cycles.  Its air stability and all-climate properties are investigated, and its potential as the sodium host in full cells has been studied.  A remarkably low volume change of 4.0% is observed.  Its high sodium diffusion coefficient has been measured and analysed via first-principles calculations, and its three-dimensional sodium ion diffusion pathways are identified.  Our results indicate that this low-cost and environmentally friendly Na4Fe3(PO4)2(P2O7)/C nanocomposite could be a competitive candidate material for sodium ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201908918"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ge, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Qiao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Bao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Enabling Superior Electrochemical Properties for Highly Efficient Potassium Storage by Impregnating Ultrafine Sb Nanocrystals within Nanochannel-Containing Carbon Nanofibers</span>. <i>Angew.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Enabling Superior Electrochemical Properties for Highly Efficient Potassium Storage by Impregnating Ultrafine Sb Nanocrystals within Nanochannel-Containing Carbon Nanofibers</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ge, Xufang; Liu, Shuhu; Qiao, Man; Du, Yichen; Li, Yafei; Bao, Jianchun; Zhou, Xiaosi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Angewandte Chemie, International Edition</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sb-based nanocomposites are attractive anode materials for batteries as they exhibit large theor. capacity and impressive working voltage.  However, tardy potassium ion diffusion characteristics, unstable Sb/electrolyte interphase, and huge vol. variation pose a challenge, hindering their practical use for potassium-ion batteries (PIBs).  Now, a simple robust strategy is presented for uniformly impregnating ultrasmall Sb nanocrystals within carbon nanofibers contg. an array of hollow nanochannels (denoted u-Sb@CNFs), resolving the issues above and yielding high-performance PIBs. u-Sb@CNFs can be directly employed as an anode, thereby dispensing with the need for conductive additives and binders.  Such a judiciously crafted u-Sb@CNF-based anode renders a set of intriguing electrochem. properties, representing large charge capacity, unprecedented cycling stability, and outstanding rate performance.  A reversible capacity of 225 mAh g-1 is retained after 2000 cycles at 1 A g-1.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201701968"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, J.-Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, P.-F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.-P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Energy/Power and Low-Temperature Cathode for Sodium-Ion Batteries: In Situ XRD Study and Superior Full-Cell Performance</span>. <i>Adv.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Org. electrodes have attracted significant attention as alternatives to conventional inorg. electrodes in terms of sustainability and universal availability in natural systems.  However, low working voltages and low energy densities are inherent limitations in cathode applications.  Here, we propose a high-energy org. cathode using a quinone-deriv., C6Cl4O2, for use in sodium-ion batteries, which boasts one of the highest av. voltages among org. electrodes in sodium batteries (∼2.72 V vs Na/Na+).  It also utilizes a two-electron transfer to provide an energy of 580 Wh kg-1.  D. functional theory (DFT) calcns. reveal that the introduction of electroneg. elements into the quinone structure significantly increased the sodium storage potential and thus enhanced the energy d. of the electrode, the latter being substantially higher than previously known quinone-derived cathodes.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Tin disulfide (SnS2) is a promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries because of its high specific capacity. Herein, a multistep templating method has been exploited for the rational design and synthesis of SnS2 nanosheets confined in carbon nanotubes (SnS2@CNTs).  To demonstrate the universality of this method, SnS2 nanosheets confined in carbon nanoboxes (SnS2@CNBs) and hollow carbon nanospheres (SnS2@CNSs) have also been synthesized by simply changing the template in the reaction system.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Carbon Quantum Dot Surface-Engineered VO2 Interwoven Nanowires: A Flexible Cathode Material for Lithium and Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Balogun, Muhammad-Sadeeq; Luo, Yang; Lyu, Feiyi; Wang, Fuxin; Yang, Hao; Li, Haibo; Liang, Chaolun; Huang, Miao; Huang, Yongchao; Tong, Yexiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">15</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">9733-9744</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The use of electrode materials in their powdery form requires binders and conductive additives for the fabrication of the cells, which leads to unsatisfactory energy storage performance.  Recently, a new strategy to design flexible, binder-, and additive-free three-dimensional electrodes with nanoscale surface engineering has been exploited in boosting the storage performance of electrode materials.  In this paper, we design a new type of free-standing carbon quantum dot coated VO2 interwoven nanowires through a simple fabrication process and demonstrate its potential to be used as cathode material for lithium and sodium ion batteries.  The versatile carbon quantum dots that are vastly flexible for surface engineering serve the function of protecting the nanowire surface and play an important role in the diffusion of electrons.  Also, the three-dimensional carbon cloth coated with VO2 interwoven nanowires assisted in the diffusion of ions through the inner and the outer surface.  With this unique architecture, the carbon quantum dot nanosurface engineered VO2 electrode exhibited capacities of 420 and 328 mAh g-1 at c.d. rate of 0.3 C for lithium and sodium storage, resp.  This work serves as a milestone for the potential replacement of lithium ion batteries and next generation postbatteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201502217"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yu, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lou, X.                         cas_cit17R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">17</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Sustainable Route from Biomass Byproduct Okara to High Content Nitrogen-Doped Carbon Sheets for Efficient Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Tingzhou; Qian, Tao; Wang, Mengfan; Shen, Xiaowei; Xu, Na; Sun, Zhouzhou; Yan, Chenglin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A novel concept for synthesizing N-doped carbon sheets (NDCS) is presented which uses okara as the source of both carbon and nitrogen, yielding a higher content of nitrogen (about 9.89 at%) than previous methods.  Okara was treated with a carbonization process followed by exfoliation.  The exfoliation process could expand the space of carbon sheets and endow the prodn. higher surface area, which benefits the intercalation and deintercalation of sodium ions during the cycle process.  As compared to bare carbon materials, both ion and electron diffusion of the N-doped carbon-based materials are enhanced, resulting in high electronic cond.  As the anodes of sodium ion batteries, the prepd. NDCS exhibits exceptionally stable capacity retention and high capacities.  It is believed that NDCS is a promising candidate for the low-cost, large-scale com. prodn. of sodium ion battery electrode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref18"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref18" class="refNumLink">18</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit18" data-doi="10.1002/anie.201510978"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zheng, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mao, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Z.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Boosted Charge Transfer in SnS/SnO<sub>2</sub> Heterostructures: Toward High Rate Capability for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Inorg.                         cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Solution-Processed Two-Dimensional Metal Dichalcogenide-Based Nanomaterials for Energy Storage and Conversion</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Cao, Xiehong; Tan, Chaoliang; Zhang, Xiao; Zhao, Wei; Zhang, Hua</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Materials (Weinheim, Germany)</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is presented.  The development of renewable energy storage and conversion devices is one of the most promising ways to address the current energy crisis, along with the global environmental concern.  The exploration of suitable active materials is the key factor for the construction of highly efficient, highly stable, low-cost and environmentally friendly energy storage and conversion devices.  The ability to prep. two-dimensional (2D) metal dichalcogenide (MDC) nanosheets and their functional composites in high yield and large scale via various soln.-based methods in recent years has inspired great research interests in their utilization for renewable energy storage and conversion applications.  Here, we will summarize the recent advances of soln.-processed 2D MDCs and their hybrid nanomaterials for energy storage and conversion applications, including rechargeable batteries, supercapacitors, electrocatalytic hydrogen generation and solar cells.  Moreover, based on the current progress, we will also give some personal insights on the existing challenges and future research directions in this promising field.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref25" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1002/chem.201701691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lu, H.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Uniform Yolk-Shell MoS<sub>2</sub>@Carbon Microsphere Anodes for High-Performance Lithium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem.—Eur.                         cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Uniform Yolk-Shell MoS2@Carbon Microsphere Anodes for High-Performance Lithium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Pan, Yunmei; Zhang, Jiajia; Lu, Hongbin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry - A European Journal</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">23</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">41</span>),
